import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a transposed 1D convolution operation with asymmetric input and square kernel.
    Supports padding, striding, and dilation.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the square convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv1d_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the transposed 1D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, length).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, length_out).
        """
        return self.conv1d_transpose(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = 3
# long sequence
length = 131072
stride = 2
padding = 1
dilation = 2

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, length)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

Actually, there's an even better approach: Transpose conv with stride 2 can be implemented by reshaping the input, doing a standard conv, and reshaping the output. Specifically, since stride=2 and we insert zeros, an approach called "sub-pixel convolution" or "pixel shuffle" style. But for 1D: If we view the input as (batch, in_channels) x length, and we want to interleave with zeros and then convolve... actually, K=3, stride=2, and the non-zero outputs are at odd positions with q=1..131072 having a full conv pattern. Hmm, a particularly efficient approach is to use CUTLASS or a GEMM-based method, but that's very complex to write inline. Given that K is only 3, the most practical custom kernel is direct computation. And since the operation is equivalent to a very simple gather pattern for the non-zero outputs, we can write a kernel that computes all output positions (including zeros) with each thread handling a block of output positions. Let me reconsider the write coalescing. If each thread handles a contiguous segment of output positions, and writes them serially, then different threads write to different segments. This is coalesced across threads if segments are large? No, threads don't access the same cache line simultaneously in this case. But serial writes within a thread have good memory throughput. Actually, for global memory writes, having each thread write many consecutive words is very efficient. So we should assign contiguous output positions to each thread. Here's a good assignment: - Grid: (batch, out_channel, num_blocks_per_channel) - Block: threads_per_block threads - Total non-zero positions per (b, oc): 131072 - We can process ALL output positions (262145) per (b, oc). - num_blocks_per_channel = ceil(262145 / (threads_per_block * items_per_thread)) Each thread processes `items_per_thread` consecutive output positions (e.g., 8 or 16). This means each thread does: for i in range(items_per_thread): l = block_start_l + tid * items_per_thread + i compute out[b, oc, l] using the gather formula. But computing even l is trivial (write 0), and for odd l we need the summation. However, if items_per_thread is large (e.g., 16), and a warp has 32 threads, then warp processes 512 consecutive l values. That's 2KB of output. That's well within cache line alignment. For input reads: adjacent l values in [a, a+511] correspond to q values that are mostly adjacent (for the odd l's, q = (l+1)/2). But the pattern of valid k changes slowly. For a block of 512 consecutive l values, 256 are odd. For each odd l, we need input at q, q-1, q-2. The q values span roughly 256 consecutive input positions. This read pattern is very friendly for L1 cache - once loaded, a cache line of input serves multiple q values. With this block-based approach, memory behavior is very efficient. Let's estimate thread count: 262145 output positions per (b, oc). We want to launch enough blocks to saturate the GPU. 1024 (b, oc) pairs. If each (b, oc) is one block, that's 1024 blocks. A100 has 108 SMs, so ~9 blocks per SM. Each block could have 512 threads. That's 4608 threads per SM, which is good considering A100 supports up to 2048 threads/SM (1024 for some configs). Wait, A100 supports 2048 threads/SM. 512 threads per block * 9 blocks = 4608 > 2048. So some blocks are waiting. Actually occupancy would be limited. Let me get GPU specs to design appropriately.

A transposed convolution with kernel_size=K, stride=S, padding=P, dilation=D can be thought of as: 1. Let me write a kernel that works generally but has a fast path for our specific case, or simply write an efficient general kernel. **General kernel strategy:** Since the kernel_size is small (3), we can use the "scatter" approach where each block processes a tile of input elements and accumulates into output positions using shared memory. Actually, for a general conv_transpose1d with small kernel, here's a good parallel strategy: Divide the output into tiles along the length dimension (e.g., 128 or 256 positions per tile). For each tile, determine which input positions contribute to it. Load the required input and weights into shared memory. Compute the tile's output and write it. But the problem is that an input position can contribute to multiple tiles, making it hard to assign independent work without atomics. Alternative approach: Process in "wavefronts". For a given stride S and dilation D, inputs that are S positions apart in the input write to non-overlapping output positions if the kernel fits within the gap. Gap between output positions from input i and input i+S: Input i writes at base 0, D, 2D, ..., (K-1)D Input i+S writes at base S, S+D, S+2D, ..., S+(K-1)D For no overlap: S > (K-1)*D, i.e., S > 2*2 = 4. But S=2 < 4, so they always overlap. Since S < (K-1)*D, there's always overlap between adjacent inputs. What if we process every S-th input element? Inputs i, i+S, i+2S, ...: Input i: 0, D, 2D, ..., (K-1)D (relative to i*S) Input i+S: S, S+D, ..., S+(K-1)D These overlap! Specifically, S = 2, and D = 2. Input i contributes at 0, 2, 4. Input i+2 contributes at 4, 6, 8. They overlap at 4. What about every T-th input where T = ceil(((K-1)*D + 1) / S)? Here that's ceil(5/2) = 3. Input i: 0, 2, 4 Input i+3: 6, 8, 10 No overlap! Since 4 < 6. So if we process inputs in 3 separate phases: phase 0 handles i=0,3,6,...; phase 1 handles i=1,4,7,...; phase 2 handles i=2,5,8,..., then within each phase, writes don't overlap, and we don't need atomics! This is a great insight. For our parameters, we need 3 phases. Each phase handles about 1/3 of the inputs. Within a phase, multiple threads can process different input elements and write to distinct output positions without conflicts. Let's verify: input i and i+3. i writes to (2i-1, 2i+1, 2i+3) i+3 writes to (2i+5, 2i+7, 2i+9) Max of i: 2i+3 < 2i+5 = min of i+3. No overlap. ✓ So we can launch 3 kernel calls (or one kernel with 3 phases) where in each phase we process non-overlapping inputs. Within a phase, each thread processes one (b, ic, i) and updates K output positions for all oc. Since no other thread in the phase writes to the same output position, we don't need atomics. This is very efficient! And it generalizes: the number of phases is ceil(((K-1)*D + 1) / S). For our case: ceil((2*2 + 1) / 2) = ceil(2.5) = 3 phases. But we also need to handle the channel dimension. For each (b, ic, i), we need to update output[b, :, output_pos] with input[b, ic, i] * weight[ic, :, k]. Since weight[ic, oc, k] is 1D in oc for fixed ic and k, and output[b, :, pos] is a vector, we can have each thread compute a vector of length OC=64. Within a warp, different threads handle different (b, ic, i) but potentially different output positions. This is fine. Wait, but all threads in a warp need to access weights[ic, :, k] where ic varies per thread. If we use shared memory for weights, we need to load weights for all ic used in the block. For a block processing a subset of (b, ic, i) pairs, if we group by ic, then all threads in a block have the same ic, so they all load the same weight[ic, :, k]. Then each thread has a different i (and possibly different b). Actually, a block can handle one ic at a time. For fixed ic, we process many (b, i) pairs in parallel. But if one block only handles one ic, then for IC=32, we'd need at least 32 blocks to cover all channels. With B=16 and L_in=131072, total work per ic is 16 * 131072 = 2,097,152 elements. If each block has 256 threads and each thread processes one (b, i), then each block handles 256 elements. For 2M elements, we need ~8192 blocks per ic. That's plenty. And since each block handles only one ic, we can load weight[ic, 0:OC, 0:K] into shared memory (or registers). Weight per ic: 64*3*4 = 768 bytes. This easily fits in shared memory. Actually, we can store weight[ic, :, :] in registers or shared memory for the block. Since each thread needs the same weight for the same ic, we can load it once to shared memory. Then each thread reads input[b, ic, i] (one float), computes for k=0,1,2: input_val * weight[ic, oc, k] for all oc, and writes to output[b, oc, output_pos]. Wait, if each thread computes all OC=64 values for one (b, i), that's 64 output writes per thread. Since the output writes for different threads in a block go to different positions (within a phase), they're non-overlapping. But the output writes within a single thread go to K different positions (for K=3). So each thread does: 1. Read input (1 float) 2. For k=0..K-1: - Compute output_pos = i*S + k*D - P - For oc=0..OC-1: output[b, oc, output_pos] += input_val * weight[ic, oc, k] This requires output to be in some memory space, and each thread writes to K*OC = 192 floats = 768 bytes. With 256 threads per block, that's 256 * 768 = 196 KB of writes per block, which is reasonable. But all these writes are scattered in memory because output_pos varies. Wait, for a fixed (b, ic) and different i within the same phase, the output positions are spread out. For phase 0 (i=0,3,6,...): i=0: positions -1, 1, 3 -> only 1, 3 valid i=3: positions 5, 7, 9 etc. Different threads write to different output positions. For a given block, if threads handle consecutive i values, they write to positions that are separated by 3*stride = 6 in the output. But along the oc dimension, it's contiguous if we write carefully. Actually, output layout is (B, OC, L_out). For fixed b and oc, position l is at offset (b * OC + oc) * L_out + l. If thread t writes to positions l_0, l_1, l_2 for t, and thread t+1 writes to l_0+6, l_1+6, l_2+6, then the memory access pattern is strided by 6 floats along L_out. This is not great for coalescing. But we can have each warp write to consecutive output positions if we assign threads differently. Actually, since L_out = 262145 and OC=64, for fixed b and oc, the stride between adjacent positions in L_out is 1 float. If different threads in a warp write to positions that differ by 6, that's not fully coalesced. But with H100's advanced memory subsystem, non-coalesced writes might still be reasonably fast, especially for small strides. An alternative: have each thread handle one (b, oc, i), computing the K contributions to a single output position... but then different ic's would conflict on the same output position. Actually, what if we change the parallelization? Instead of each thread doing a (b, ic, i) and outputting to all oc, we can have each thread do (b, oc, i_phase) where it only computes one oc but multiple ic contributions? For a fixed phase and fixed output position, the contributing inputs are a few specific i values. A thread can sum over ic to compute the final output for one (b, oc, o). Let me reconsider the phase approach but with output-centric parallelization. In phase p, we process inputs i = p, p+3, p+6, ... Each such input writes to output positions: 2i-1, 2i+1, 2i+3. For phase 0: positions 1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, ... (all odd positions, specifically every 2nd odd position for each i, but i increments by 3 so positions increment by 6) Wait, i=0: 1, 3 i=3: 5, 7, 9 i=6: 11, 13, 15 Actually for phase p, the positions are 2(p+3t)-1, 2(p+3t)+1, 2(p+3t)+3 for t=0,1,2,... = 2p+6t-1, 2p+6t+1, 2p+6t+3 For p=0: 6t-1, 6t+1, 6t+3 = -1, 1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, 13, 15, ... For p=1: 6t+1, 6t+3, 6t+5 = 1, 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, ... For p=2: 6t+3, 6t+5, 6t+7 = 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, ... So the phases cover overlapping sets of output positions. The phases together cover all odd positions, with each position getting contributions from exactly the right set of (i, k) pairs. But from the perspective of a single phase, positions from different t values are separated by 6. Within a phase, no two inputs write to the same position, but different phases write to the same positions. So to avoid atomics across phases, we could: 1. Run phase 0, write to output 2. Run phase 1, add to output 3. Run phase 2, add to output But phase 1 and phase 2 need to add to positions that phase 0 already wrote to. Actually, if each phase writes to its own temporary buffer and we sum them at the end, that requires 3x memory. Or if we accumulate in shared memory and write out in one go. Wait, maybe a simpler approach: since we only have 3 phases with significant overlap, maybe we can assign warps/threads to output positions directly. Let me think about a thread-centric approach: Each thread computes one (b, oc, o) where o is an odd output position. It sums over k=0..K-1, computing the corresponding input position and reading input + weight. For our parameters, each output position has at most 3 contributing (i, k) pairs. So each thread does at most 3 loads from input, 3 loads from weight, and computes a sum. Total threads: B * OC * num_odd_positions = 16 * 64 * 131072 = 134,217,728 threads. At 32 threads/warp, that's 4,194,304 warps. At 132 SMs, that's 31,775 warps per SM. Each warp does about 3 * 32 = 96 loads from input, 96 loads from weight, and 96 FMAs. The loads from input: different threads in a warp access different (b, o), which might mean different i values. For a warp of 32 threads processing the same b and consecutive oc and the same o... or same (b, o) and consecutive oc? A warp of 32 threads can compute this GEMV efficiently by having each thread handle 2 oc values. Processing: for each k contributing to o: 1. For each oc handled by thread t (2 oc values): For ic=0..31: accum[oc] += input_shared[ic] * weight[ic, oc, k] Weight access pattern: for fixed k, weight[0:32, oc, k] where oc varies per thread. Threads in a warp access different oc. For fixed ic, they all read weight[ic, different_oc, k]. If weight is stored as (IC, OC, K), then weight[ic, oc, k] is at offset (ic*OC + oc)*K + k. So for fixed ic, threads access consecutive oc values -> consecutive memory. Coalesced! And input is broadcast (same for all threads in warp). This is very efficient! Each warp computes one (b, o) pair for all oc. Total warps: B * num_odd_positions = 16 * 131072 = 2,097,152 warps. Each warp computes 3 GEMVs of size 32x64, effectively 3 * 32 * 64 = 6144 FMAs. The occupancy: 2M warps / 132 SMs ≈ 15,887 warps per SM. The H100 can handle thousands of concurrent warps per SM, but the limit is typically 64 warps per SM for 32-thread warps (depending on registers/shared memory). Let's check: 64 warps/SM * 132 SMs = 8,448 concurrent warps max. So 2M warps would take at least 249 rounds. But each round is very fast. Actually, with sufficient independent warps, the GPU can hide latency. But 15.9K warps per SM might exceed the hardware capacity. The warps will be queued. Actually let's estimate: each thread holds a few floats for accumulators (2), input index, loop counters, etc. Maybe 20-30 registers. Each iteration takes the time to execute one warp, which is maybe 50-100 cycles for 6144 FMAs (at 2 FMAs per cycle per thread, 32 threads -> 64 FMAs/cycle -> ~100 cycles). Plus memory loads. Actually the memory loads are the bottleneck. Also, I realize that in PyTorch, ConvTranspose1d weights might actually be stored differently from how I think. Let me check: in PyTorch, the weight shape for ConvTranspose1d is (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size). The computation involves cross-correlation with the transposed kernel, which means the indexing might be reversed. Actually, PyTorch ConvTranspose1d corresponds to: output = conv_transpose1d(input, weight, bias, stride, padding, output_padding, groups, dilation) The mathematical formula (from PyTorch docs) is: out(N_i, C_out, l) = bias(C_out) + sum_{k=0}^{C_in-1} weight(C_out, k, @) \star input(N_i, k) where \star is the cross-correlation operator. But for transposed convolution, this is the transpose of conv. Actually, PyTorch's documentation for ConvTranspose1d says: "This module can be seen as the gradient of Conv1d with respect to its input." And the shape formula is: L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 And looking at the actual implementation: there are different algorithms, but the default calls cuDNN's conv transpose. For cuDNN conv transpose, it internally implements it as: - The transpose of a regular convolution - Or equivalently, as a regular convolution with modified parameters on a dilated input Specifically, one common implementation is: 1. Create dilated input: zeros of size (L_in - 1) * stride + 1 2. Place input values at positions 0, stride, 2*stride, ... 3. Apply regular convolution with kernel_size = (K-1)*dilation + 1, stride=1, padding = (K-1)*dilation - padding Wait, for our parameters: Dilated input size = (131072 - 1) * 2 + 1 = 262143. Place inputs at positions 0, 2, 4, ..., 262142. Regular conv with K_eff = (3-1)*2 + 1 = 5, stride=1, padding = 2*2 - 1 = 3. Output size = 262143 + 2*0 - 5 + 1 = 262139? No wait. Actually regular conv output: (L + 2*P - D*(K-1) - 1)/S + 1 If we treat dilated input length as 262143, and use regular conv with K=5, S=1, P=3: (262143 + 6 - 4 - 1)/1 + 1 = 262145. Yes! And the regular conv weights would be spread out according to dilation. So weight positions 0, 2, 4 correspond to the original kernel elements. In this formulation, the transposed conv is equivalent to: - Dilate the input by inserting stride-1 zeros between elements - Apply standard conv with kernel_size_eff = (K-1)*dilation+1, stride=1, padding_eff = (K-1)*dilation - padding For our params: input size 262143, K_eff=5, P_eff=3. The standard conv accesses positions: i + k - 3 for k=0..4. But inputs are only at even positions (0, 2, 4, ...). So for output position j: j = i + k - 3 where i is even. => j = even + k - 3. For k=0: j = even - 3 = odd For k=1: j = even - 2 = even For k=2: j = even - 1 = odd For k=3: j = even = even For k=4: j = even + 1 = odd So some output positions at even j do get contributions! For example: Output j=0: from i=2, k=3 (i is even input position, k=3 in the effective kernel). But wait, does the original kernel have k=3? No, the original kernel has K=3 elements, but the effective kernel has 5 positions with the original 3 elements at positions 0, 2, 4 (for dilation=2). Actually in the effective kernel, the weights are at positions 0, 2, 4 corresponding to original k=0,1,2. Positions 1 and 3 are zero. So for j=0: i + k_eff - 3 = 0. Possible (i, k_eff): - i=2 (input position 2), k_eff=1 -> weight at eff pos 1 is 0! - i=4, k_eff=-1 -> invalid - i=0, k_eff=3 -> weight at eff pos 3 is 0! - i=2, k_eff=1 -> 0 weight So j=0 gets 0 contribution because the effective kernel positions 1 and 3 are zero! Similarly, j=2: i + k_eff - 3 = 2. - i=0, k_eff=5 -> invalid - i=2, k_eff=3 -> eff pos 3 has 0 weight! - i=4, k_eff=1 -> eff pos 1 has 0 weight! So j=2 also gets 0. In general, for output position j, if j is even, then i + k_eff = j + P_eff = j + 3. Since i is even, k_eff must be odd. But the effective kernel has non-zero weights only at even positions (0, 2, 4). So k_eff is even, meaning j+3 must be even, so j must be odd. Therefore, only odd j have non-zero output! This confirms my earlier analysis. Great! So a large fraction (about half) of the output is exactly zero. **Kernel Design** Given the parameters are fixed for the main test, I'll write a kernel that's optimized for these specific parameters but general enough to handle other cases too. My approach: 1. Check at runtime if the parameters result in a sparse output pattern. But actually, to keep it simple and fast, I'll just write the kernel for the general case and add an optimization for when we can skip positions. 2. Better yet, write a general kernel that processes all output positions, but for each position, checks if there are any valid contributions. If not, skip. However, that check adds overhead. For our specific case, we know only odd positions have contributions. We can compute only those positions. Let me think about the most efficient implementation: **Option A: Scatter input to output (3 phases, no atomics)** - 3 kernel launches (phases) - Each phase processes inputs i = phase, phase+3, phase+6, ... - Within a phase, each thread reads input[b, ic, i] and writes to K output positions - Since no overlap within a phase, no atomics needed - But writes to output are scattered - And we need to either zero-initialize output or have a first phase that writes and others that add To avoid read-modify-write, we can have each phase write to a separate temporary buffer of size B*OC*L_out, then sum the 3 buffers. But that uses 3x output memory. Alternatively, if we know the exact pattern, we can assign each output position to exactly one phase! But for our parameters, each valid output position receives contributions from up to 3 different phases. So we can't. Unless... for our parameters, output position o=2m-1 receives from: - k=0: i=m -> phase = m%3 - k=1: i=m-1 -> phase = (m-1)%3 - k=2: i=m-2 -> phase = (m-2)%3 So a single output position gets contributions from 3 different phases. We could process all 3 contributions in one thread by having the thread read all necessary inputs. That's the "gather" approach. **Option B: Gather output from input ( warp per (b, o) )** - Each warp computes one (b, o) for all oc - No atomics needed - Each warp reads up to K input positions and K weight matrices - Writes to K different output positions (actually just 1 output position with all oc values) Wait, if a warp computes all oc for a single (b, o), it writes to output[b, 0:OC, o]. That's a contiguous vector of 64 floats for fixed b and o. This is very coalesced! And the computation for each (b, o) is independent. This seems like the best approach. Let me detail it: For each output position o that potentially has non-zero value: For each b: Warp of 32 threads computes output[b, :, o] The warp loads: - For each k=0..K-1 and each ic=0..IC-1: input[b, ic, i_k] (same for all threads in warp) - For each k and each thread's oc values: weight[ic, oc, k] Since each thread handles 2 oc values (64/32=2), the warp computes all 64 outputs. Computation for thread t (oc = 2*t and 2*t+1): accum_0 = 0, accum_1 = 0 For each k: i = (o + padding - k*dilation) / stride if valid: For ic = 0..31: in_val = input[b, ic, i] accum_0 += in_val * weight[ic, 2*t, k] accum_1 += in_val * weight[ic, 2*t+1, k] output[b, 2*t, o] = accum_0 + bias[2*t] output[b, 2*t+1, o] = accum_1 + bias[2*t+1] Wait, but if we iterate over all o including even ones, half the warps will find no valid k and immediately return zero. That's a lot of wasted work. To optimize, I should only launch warps for valid o values. For our parameters, valid o are odd positions from 1 to 262143. How to map block/warp indices to valid o values efficiently? If I use one block per (b, o) pair, with block having 32 threads (one warp): Grid: (B, num_valid_o) Each block does one (b, o_valid). For our parameters, num_valid_o = 131072. Grid: (16, 131072). Total blocks: 2,097,152. But CUDA has a grid dimension limit. The y dimension max is 65535 for some GPUs. On H100 it's 2^31-1 for x and y? Actually on modern CUDA, gridDim.x, y, z limits are high. x, y up to 2^31-1, z up to 65535. So (16, 131072) is fine. Alternatively, I can flatten: grid.x = B * num_valid_o, grid.y = 1. With 2M blocks and 32 threads each, we have 67M threads. On H100 with 132 SMs, each SM processes roughly 15.9K blocks. With one warp per block, this is manageable. But is it efficient to have 2M blocks? Block launch overhead might be noticeable but probably small on H100. Actually, having 2M blocks means the GPU has to track many blocks. The hardware has a limit on how many blocks can be queued. But with 32 threads per block (one warp), the occupancy should be high because we can fit many warps per SM. Wait, each block has only one warp. On H100, max 32 blocks per SM (with limited threads per block). Actually, each SM on H100 can have at most 32 thread blocks. So with 132 SMs, max concurrent blocks = 132 * 32 = 4,224. Within a block of 256 threads (8 warps), each warp handles one (b, o). Warps within a block are independent. But we need to be careful: if we have 256 threads, they need to be grouped into warps. Let me think about another approach: process multiple oc per thread, or multiple b's, to increase work per thread. If each thread processes one (b, o, oc_chunk), where oc_chunk is 2 consecutive oc values, then 32 threads = one warp processes one (b, o) for all 64 oc. And we can have 8 warps per block, each handling a different (b, o). Total blocks = (B * num_valid_o) / 8. For our params: 2,097,152 / 8 = 262,144 blocks. Good. In shared memory, we can preload input[b, :, i_k] for the 8 different (b, o) pairs handled by the block. But the input indices i_k depend on o, and different warps in the block have different o. If we preload in shared memory, we'd need space for 8 * 3 * 32 floats = 768 floats ≈ 3 KB. Very manageable! Similarly, weights can be preloaded into shared memory. Weight size: 32 * 64 * 3 = 6144 floats ≈ 24 KB. Total shared memory: ~27 KB. Well within limits. This would make the kernel very efficient: 1. Load weights to shared memory (all warps cooperate, maybe via vectorized loads) 2. Each warp loads the 3 input vectors (each 32 floats) it needs into a small shared memory region or registers 3. Compute GEMV using shared memory weights 4. Write output Actually, since weights are shared across ALL blocks and ALL warps, they should naturally stay in L2 cache. But loading them from L2 to shared memory within a block avoids repeated L2 accesses. Hmm, but weights are 24 KB, and L2 cache is 50 MB, so multiple blocks' weights requests can all hit L2. Loading weights from L2 to registers is probably fast enough. We might not need shared memory for weights. For inputs: each warp needs up to 3 vectors of 32 floats. If threads in a warp read the same input values, there's potential for broadcast from L1/L2. But actually, in the GEMV each thread needs the same input values at different times, so we should load them once and broadcast. Let me sketch the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose1d_fast( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int IC, int OC, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int S, int P, int D ) { // Block handles multiple (b, o) pairs int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; int pair_id = blockIdx.x * num_warps + warp_id; int total_pairs = B * L_out; // or num_valid_o if (pair_id >= total_pairs) return; int b = pair_id / L_out; int o = pair_id % L_out; // Check if this output position has any contribution // For general case: iterate k and find valid inputs // For optimized case: skip even positions float accum0 = 0.0f, accum1 = 0.0f; // each thread handles 2 oc for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int numerator = o + P - k * D; if (numerator % S != 0) continue; int i = numerator / S; if (i < 0 || i >= L_in) continue; // Each thread handles oc = 2*lane and 2*lane+1 int oc0 = 2 * lane; int oc1 = 2 * lane + 1; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++) { float in_val = input[(b * IC + ic) * L_in + i]; float w0 = weight[(ic * OC + oc0) * K + k]; float w1 = weight[(ic * OC + oc1) * K + k]; accum0 += in_val * w0; accum1 += in_val * w1; } } // Write output int out_base = (b * OC) * L_out + o; if (oc0 < OC) output[out_base + oc0 * L_out] = accum0 + (bias ? bias[oc0] : 0); if (oc1 < OC) output[out_base + oc1 * L_out] = accum1 + (bias ? bias[oc1] : 0); } ``` Wait, the output layout for ConvTranspose1d: output shape is (B, OC, L_out), and it's contiguous in L_out. So: output[b, oc, l] = output[(b * OC + oc) * L_out + l] In my code above: out_base = (b * OC) * L_out + o Then output for oc0 is at out_base + oc0 * L_out. When we write from a warp, different threads write to: out_base + 0*L_out, out_base + 2*L_out, out_base + 4*L_out, ... for lane 0,1,2,... The stride between consecutive thread writes is 2*L_out = 524,290 floats. That's not coalesced at all! The writes are 2 MB apart! Oh no, I made a critical error. The memory layout is (B, OC, L_out), so all L_out values for a given (b, oc) are contiguous. But different oc's are separated by L_out floats. If thread 0 writes to oc=0 and thread 1 writes to oc=2, they're separated by 2*L_out, which is massive. For coalesced writes within a warp, we want consecutive threads to write to consecutive memory locations. So what if we assign lanes to consecutive oc values? Lane 0: oc=0,1 Lane 1: oc=2,3 ... Lane 31: oc=62,63 Then writes are at: out_base + 0*L_out, out_base + 2*L_out, ..., out_base + 62*L_out. Still not consecutive! Consecutive threads write to addresses separated by 2*L_out. Actually, in this layout, there's no way for threads writing different oc values to have coalesced access. The only way to get coalescing is if we write one oc at a time (not parallel) or if we transpose the output. Wait, but 32 threads in a warp can all write to the same memory address? No, they write to different addresses. The question is whether those addresses fall within the same cache line. On H100, cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. If lane 0 writes to out_base + 0*L_out and lane 1 writes to out_base + 2*L_out, with L_out = 262145, the distance is 524,290 floats ≈ 2,097,160 bytes. These are in completely different cache lines. So each warp generates 32 separate cache line write requests. That's terrible! To get coalescing, we need consecutive threads to write to consecutive memory. With layout (B, OC, L_out), for a fixed (b, o), as oc increases by 1, the address increases by L_out. So consecutive oc values are NOT consecutive in memory. The only way to get coalescing is to have consecutive threads write to consecutive o values for the SAME oc. Or to transpose the output. Let me reconsider the parallelization. **Alternative parallelization: Thread per (b, oc, o)** Each thread computes one float: output[b, oc, o]. This is fully parallel and writes are coalesced if we process consecutive o for the same (b, oc). How many threads? B * OC * num_valid_o = 16 * 64 * 131072 = 134,217,728. In groups of 32: 4,194,304 warps. If each block has 256 threads (8 warps), we need 524,288 blocks. Within a warp, if consecutive threads process consecutive o for the same (b, oc), then writes are coalesced! Because consecutive o are consecutive in memory. For a fixed (b, oc), threads in a warp process o = o_start, o_start+1, o_start+2, ..., o_start+31. But only odd o values have non-zero contributions. For a warp of 32 threads, the addresses span 31 * 8 = 248 bytes. A cache line is 128 bytes, so two cache lines cover 32 threads writing 8 bytes apart. With 32-bit floats, each thread writes 4 bytes, spaced 8 bytes apart. This means each pair of threads fits in 8 bytes, and 32 threads need 256 bytes (2 cache lines). But since each thread only writes 4 bytes, we're writing half of each 8-byte pair. Actually, modern GPUs handle this reasonably well due to sub-cache-line writes. But it's not perfectly coalesced. Let me detail the kernel: ```cuda #define TILE_O 64 #define TILE_OC 4 #define IC 32 #define OC 64 #define K 3 __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* output, int B, int L_in, int L_out, int S, int P, int D ) { __shared__ float s_input[TILE_O][IC][K]; // or s_input[k][ic][o] __shared__ float s_weight[TILE_OC][IC][K]; // or just load on the fly int b = blockIdx.y; int oc_tile = blockIdx.x * TILE_OC; int o_tile = blockIdx.z * TILE_O; int t = threadIdx.x; // 0..127 (4 warps * 32 threads; or we can use 256 threads for 8 warps * 32) // Wait, if TILE_OC=4 and each warp handles one oc, we need 4 warps = 128 threads. // Each warp has 32 threads handling 32 consecutive o values. int warp_id = t / 32; int lane = t % 32; int oc = oc_tile + warp_id; if (oc >= OC) return; // o values handled by this thread int num_o_per_warp = TILE_O / 1; // If TILE_O=64 and we have 2 warps per oc, each warp handles 32 o. // Actually, we should have warp_id in [0, 3] for 4 oc values. // For each oc, we might want multiple warps to cover all o. // Say TILE_O = 64, and we have 2 warps per oc -> each warp handles 32 o. // Then total warps = 4 * 2 = 8 warps = 256 threads. // Let me redesign: // Threads per block = 256 = 8 warps. // TILE_OC = 4, so 4 oc groups. // For each oc group, we have 2 warps. Each warp handles 32 o. // Total o coverage: 4 * 2 * 32 = 256? No. // Simpler: TILE_O = 32, TILE_OC = 8. Then 8 warps, each handles 1 oc and 32 o. // But 8 oc per block might be too much for shared memory weights. // OK let's be more careful. // For each block, we compute output[b, oc_tile:oc_tile+TILE_OC, o_tile:o_tile+TILE_O]. int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; // Each warp has a unique (oc_local, o_subtile) // Let's say we have num_oc_warps warps per oc (handling different o ranges). // Set num_warps_per_oc = TILE_O / 32. // If TILE_O = 64, num_warps_per_oc = 2. // Total warps needed = TILE_OC * num_warps_per_oc. // If TILE_OC = 4, total warps = 8 -> 256 threads. Good. int num_warps_per_oc = TILE_O / 32; // assumes TILE_O is multiple of 32 int total_warps = TILE_OC * num_warps_per_oc; if (warp_id >= total_warps) return; int oc_local = warp_id / num_warps_per_oc; int o_warp_id = warp_id % num_warps_per_oc; int oc = oc_tile + oc_local; int o_start = o_tile + o_warp_id * 32; int o = o_start + lane; if (oc >= OC || o >= L_out) return; // Now this thread computes output[b, oc, o] float accum = bias ? bias[oc] : 0.0f; // We need to load input values for all valid (o, k) into shared memory. // But since different warps need different o and the validity depends, // maybe we just load on demand. for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int numer = o + P - k * D; if (numer % S != 0) continue; int i = numer / S; if (i < 0 || i >= L_in) continue; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++) { float in_val = input[(b * IC + ic) * L_in + i]; float w = weight[(ic * OC + oc) * K + k]; accum += in_val * w; } } output[(b * OC + oc) * L_out + o] = accum; } ``` Wait, this doesn't use shared memory at all, and each thread does its own loads. But the weight loads for the same (ic, oc, k) across threads in a warp are broadcast (same address), and the input loads are coalesced for fixed k and consecutive o (since consecutive o have consecutive i for fixed k). Also, the loop over k means each thread has 3 different i values. For the same k across the warp, the i values are consecutive, so the input loads are coalesced. For different k, the i values might be different. Let me verify: for o=1, k=0: i=1; o=3, k=0: i=2; o=5, k=0: i=3. Consecutive o differ by 1 in i. Coalesced. For k=1: o=1, i=0; o=3, i=1; o=5, i=2. Let me think about another optimization: since we know the exact validity pattern for our specific parameters, we can unroll the k loop and hardcode the validity checks. For our parameters (S=2, P=1, D=2): For a thread processing o: k=0: i = (o+1)/2. Valid if o is odd and 0 <= (o+1)/2 <= L_in-1. k=1: i = (o-1)/2. So for most interior o, all 3 k's are valid. Only near the very start and very end do we have fewer. If we use a kernel that only handles odd o, the validity checks simplify to boundary checks. A cleaner way to handle this is to process by "m" where o = 2*m - 1. Then m ranges from 1 to 131072. k=0: i = m. Valid if 0 <= m <= 131071. So m in [1, 131071]. k=1: i = m-1. Valid if 0 <= m-1 <= 131071. So m in [1, 131072]. k=2: i = m-2. Valid if 0 <= m-2 <= 131071. So m in [2, 131073], but m max is 131072, so m in [2, 131072]. For m=1: k=0 (i=1), k=1 (i=0). 2 terms. For m=131072: k=1 (i=131071), k=2 (i=131070). 2 terms. For m=2..131071: 3 terms. If the evaluation uses exactly these parameters, we could write a kernel that assumes all 3 terms are valid and just handles the boundaries with conditionals. Since the boundaries are tiny (m=1 and m=131072), the branch divergence is minimal. But to be general, let's write a kernel that works for any parameters. **Let me write the kernel now.** I'll go with the warp-per-(oc, o_chunk) design where each block has multiple warps, each warp handles one oc and 32 consecutive o values. Grid structure: - x dimension: oc_tiles (OC / 4 = 16 tiles for OC=64) - y dimension: batch (16) - z dimension: o_tiles (L_out / 64, but we'll only process valid o's) Wait, if we process only odd o, that's 131072 positions. If each block processes 64 o values (via 2 warps per oc and 4 oc per block), then we need 131072 / 64 = 2048 o tiles. But we can mix: grid has (oc_tile, b, o_tile) dimensions. Actually, to avoid the complexity of skipping positions, I'll just process all o values. Half will do nothing (early return), but the warp will be somewhat divergent. On the other hand, for even o values, all threads in the warp will determine there are no valid k's and return quickly. Wait, if a warp handles 32 consecutive o values, and our valid o values are odd (1, 3, 5, ...), then a warp handling o=0..31 will have some valid o (the odd ones) and some invalid. This creates branch divergence within the warp! Threads with invalid o return early, while others do the compute. The early-return threads sit idle. Not ideal. Instead, we should make sure each warp handles ONLY valid o values or ONLY invalid ones. Since invalid o values are exactly the even ones, and valid ones are odd (for our params), we can launch warps for only odd o's. By having o_tile index into valid positions only, we avoid divergence. For a general kernel, we need a way to map tile indices to valid positions efficiently. But for our specific test, we know the pattern. To keep it general yet efficient, I can compute the number of valid positions and map thread blocks to them. The mapping for general S, P, D is complex. But I can do it on the fly: for each output position, check if valid. Actually, for performance, I'll launch one thread per output position (o from 0 to L_out-1), and each thread checks all k. If no valid k, it writes 0 (or skips if we can ensure output is zero-initialized). This is simple and general. With 256 threads per block: - We can process 256 o values per block for one (b, oc). - Or split into multiple oc and multiple o. Here's a simple but effective design: - Block: 256 threads. - Grid: (B, OC, ceil(L_out / 256)) - Each thread computes one (b, oc, o). - Consecutive threads in a block handle consecutive o for the same (b, oc). - Writes are coalesced. - Input loads: for the same k and ic, consecutive o have related i values. For our params, consecutive o differ by 1. For fixed k and ic, i = (o + P - k*D) / S. With S=2, i changes by 1 every 2 o's. So threads in a warp processing o=0..31 have i values that are mostly non-consecutive (half invalid, half valid). But for the valid ones among consecutive threads, i is not consecutive. Actually, if thread 0 handles o=0, thread 1 handles o=1, etc. For k=0: valid o are odd. So valid threads are 1,3,5,... and their i values are 1,2,3,4,... (consecutive!). For k=1: valid o are odd, i values are 0,1,2,3,... (consecutive!). For k=2: valid o are odd >=3, i values are 0,1,2,... (consecutive, offset by 1). So among the VALID threads in a warp, the input loads are coalesced! But the INVALID threads are diverged. For k=0, half the threads are invalid and skip the inner loop. For k=2, some boundary threads are invalid. But on modern GPUs, when some threads in a warp are inactive, the active threads still execute at full throughput. And within the active threads, the memory accesses are coalesced. So the divergence cost is: each warp executes the k loop 3 times. In iteration k=0, 16 threads are active; in k=1, 16 threads active; in k=2, 15 or 16 threads active. The inactive threads don't execute. So it's like having ~16 threads doing the work, but at full SIMD width. Wait, actually when half the threads are disabled, the warp executes with the remaining threads at the same rate. The FMAs are done by the active threads, and memory bandwidth is used by active threads. The throughput is halved for that warp. But since we have plenty of warps, the SMs are still utilized. One issue: the valid threads are every OTHER thread (odds). Are their memory accesses coalesced? Thread 1 accesses i=1, thread 3 accesses i=2, thread 5 accesses i=3. In memory: input[b, ic, 1], input[b, ic, 2], input[b, ic, 3] are consecutive floats. But the threads accessing them are 1, 3, 5 - not consecutive threads. On modern GPUs, coalescing doesn't require consecutive threads! It just requires the addresses to be in the same cache line. For 32-bit floats, a 128-byte cache line holds 32 floats. If threads 1,3,5,...,31 access addresses base+1, base+2, ..., base+16, these all fall in the same cache line. The hardware can handle this! It will load one cache line (128 bytes) and distribute the relevant floats to the threads. Actually, this is even better than I thought. Modern GPUs (since Volta/Ampere) have L1 caches that support "warp-level divergent loads" well. If all accesses fall within one or two cache lines, it's almost as efficient as perfectly coalesced loads. For 16 active threads in a warp accessing 16 consecutive floats (spread across 8 consecutive thread IDs with step 2), they all fall in one 128-byte cache line (which holds 32 floats). So only ONE memory transaction is needed! This is excellent! So the simple design is actually very efficient: - One thread per (b, oc, o) - Block: 256 threads, all handling consecutive o for one (b, oc) - Grid: (B, OC, ceil(L_out/256)) - Each thread checks all k and sums valid contributions - Writes one output element Let's verify coalescing for writes: Threads in a warp: o=0,1,2,...,31 for the same (b, oc). Output addresses: base + 0, base + 1, ..., base + 31. These are perfectly consecutive. One 128-byte transaction for the warp's 32 floats. Perfect coalescing! This is the simplest and probably quite efficient. Now, can we make it even faster with shared memory? In this simple design, weights are loaded per thread: for each ic and valid k, each thread loads weight[ic, oc, k]. But all threads in a block have the same oc! So for the same ic and k, all threads load the SAME weight. This is broadcast. cuDNN/L1 handles broadcast loads very well. The first thread to load a weight brings it into L1, and subsequent threads hit L1. So weight loads are very cheap after the first. Input loads: for the same ic and k, valid threads in a warp load consecutive i values. The whole warp's accesses fall in 1-2 cache lines. Great. I think this simple design might actually be very close to optimal for this problem, and much simpler to implement correctly. Let's implement it: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int length_in, int length_out, int kernel_size, int stride, int padding, int dilation ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int oc = blockIdx.y; int o = blockIdx.z * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (b >= batch_size || oc >= out_channels || o >= length_out) return; float accum = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[oc] : 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < kernel_size; k++) { int numer = o + padding - k * dilation; if (numer % stride != 0) continue; int i = numer / stride; if (i < 0 || i >= length_in) continue; float w = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_channels; ic++) { float in_val = input[(b * in_channels + ic) * length_in + i]; w = weight[(ic * out_channels + oc) * kernel_size + k]; accum += in_val * w; } } output[(b * out_channels + oc) * length_out + o] = accum; } ``` Wait, there's a bug: `w = weight[(ic * out_channels + oc) * kernel_size + k];` should be loaded inside the ic loop. Actually, we can optimize the memory access pattern: For a thread accumulating over ic, it accesses weight for fixed oc and k, varying ic. The weight access pattern is: weight[ic * out_channels * kernel_size + oc * kernel_size + k] As ic increases by 1, the address increases by out_channels * kernel_size. For OC=64, K=3, that's 192 floats = 768 bytes. That's a large stride, so consecutive ic loads are not coalesced or cached together. But wait - all threads in the block have the same oc and k. So the first thread that loads weight[0, oc, k] brings that cache line into L1. The next weight[1, oc, k] is 768 bytes away - a different cache line. So each ic load is a new cache line transaction. With IC=32, for each valid (k, o) pair, we load 32 weights, each in a separate cache line. That's 32 transactions of 128 bytes = 4096 bytes of memory traffic for 32 floats. But wait, each cache line contains 32 floats. When we load weight[ic, oc, k], we load one float but the entire cache line (weight[ic, 0:31, :]) comes into L1. Well, not exactly - the cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. The memory layout is (IC, OC, K) = (32, 64, 3). For fixed ic, weight[ic, :, :] is contiguous and size 64*3 = 192 floats = 768 bytes. This is 6 cache lines. For ic=0: weight[0, 0:63, 0:2] is 6 cache lines. weight[0, oc, k] is in cache line number (oc*3+k)/32. So when we load weight[ic, oc, k] for varying ic: - ic=0, oc=5, k=1: in the cache line containing weight[0, 0:31, :] or weight[0, 32:63, :]. For oc=5, it's in the first 2 cache lines of ic=0. - ic=1, oc=5, k=1: in the first 2 cache lines of ic=1. These are 768 bytes apart, so different cache lines. But importantly, if we load weight[0, 5, 1], the ENTIRE cache line containing it comes into L1. That cache line contains weight[0, 0:31, 0:2] (approximately, depending on alignment). So it contains many other weight values that we might use later if we loop over oc in the block. Actually, if all threads in the block process the SAME oc but different o, they all load the SAME weight values. The first thread to load a weight brings the cache line into L1. The next threads hit L1. So within a block, weight loads are L1 hits after the first. Also, between different blocks with the same oc, weight is in L2 cache. So the weight memory traffic is not a problem. Let me think about the inner loop more carefully: ```cuda for (int ic = 0; ic < in_channels; ic++) { float in_val = input[(b * in_channels + ic) * length_in + i]; float w = weight[(ic * out_channels + oc) * kernel_size + k]; accum += in_val * w; } ``` For fixed b, ic, and different i across threads in a warp: input[(b * IC + ic) * L_in + i] for i in some range. These are contiguous or nearly contiguous in memory. One or two cache lines per warp. For fixed oc and k, and different ic: weight[(ic * OC + oc) * K + k] This is strided by OC*K = 192 floats. Each load is a different cache line. But only needed once per block due to L1 caching. Overall, I think this simple kernel is quite efficient. But wait, there's one more issue. If we launch a block for each (b, oc), with 256 threads processing 256 consecutive o values, what about the zero outputs? For our parameters, half the o values have no valid k. Those threads do the minimum work (check 3 divisibility conditions, no inner ic loop) and write 0. The write is still coalesced (writing 0 to consecutive positions). And the compute overhead for the validity check is tiny. PyTorch's cuDNN on the other hand computes EVERY output position, including zeros, using its full GEMM machinery. For our parameters, half the outputs are zero but cuDNN might still do the full computation. Actually wait, I might be wrong. If cuDNN uses the formulation of "dilate input then do regular conv", and the regular conv is done via GEMM, the GEMM naturally computes only the real outputs, but the input has zeros in it. cuDNN handles this efficiently using sparse/dense optimizations? Not necessarily - it might do the full GEMM. For a regular conv via implicit GEMM, the operation is basically: output = weight_matrix * input_matrix where weight_matrix is OC x (IC*K_eff) and input_matrix is (IC*K_eff) x L_out. The multiplication involves OC * L_out * (IC*K_eff) operations. For our effective parameters: 64 * 262145 * (32*5) = 64 * 262145 * 160 = 2,683,596,800 FMAs per batch. For B=16: 43 GFLOPs. This is 3.3x more FLOPs than our custom kernel (12.9 GFLOPs), because the effective kernel size is 5 (due to zeros from dilation) and it doesn't skip any outputs! Now, does cuDNN/PyTorch really produce zeros at even positions? Almost certainly yes, because that's what the math dictates for these parameters. So if we specifically compute only odd positions, we skip half the output writes and compute exactly what's needed. Now, can we use this to write a very efficient kernel? Yes! I'll write a kernel that maps from output index m to o = 2m - 1 (or rather, just iterate over valid outputs directly). Actually, the simplest general approach is: - Launch one thread per (b, oc, o) where o ranges over 0..L_out-1. - For invalid o (where no k satisfies the condition), the thread quickly returns 0. - For valid o, computes the sum. Since half the threads do nothing but write 0 (which is still a memory write), the bandwidth for output writes is doubled compared to computing only valid positions. If we want to squeeze more performance, we can write a kernel that specifically computes only valid outputs. But for simplicity and generality, I'll write the general kernel first and see if it's fast enough. Actually, given the test has these specific parameters, and half the output is zero, a general kernel that writes all positions might be memory-bandwidth limited on output writes. Let me write two versions: 1. A general kernel that works for all parameters, processing all output positions. 2. An optimized kernel for our specific parameters that only computes odd positions. I'll implement version 1 first, test correctness, then check performance. If it's not fast enough, I'll add the optimization. Actually, I'll start with a general kernel and see. The evaluation might include exactly these parameters, and PyTorch also has to write all positions. But PyTorch might use a highly optimized cuDNN that computes through GEMM and writes all positions in one go, which could saturate memory bandwidth. Wait, PyTorch's cuDNN ConvTranspose might not write zeros explicitly; it might compute all positions through GEMM, and some naturally come out as zero. The GEMM memory bandwidth is similar to what we'd use. But our custom kernel that checks each position individually might have more instruction overhead than a GEMM. To be safe, let me write an efficient kernel that handles the exact computation well. I'll use this design: - Block: 128 threads. - Grid: (batch_size, out_channels, ceil(L_out / 128)) - Each thread computes one (b, oc, o). This is simple, coalesced writes, and reasonable parallelism. To further optimize, I can reorder the loops: For each thread, for each k, check validity, then loop over ic. Since ic is the inner loop, and all threads in a warp have the same k and b but different i and same ic, the weight load is broadcast (same address). And consecutive ic values are strided. Actually, within a warp, for fixed k and ic, all threads load weight[ic, oc, k] (same address for all threads). This is automatically handled well by the GPU. And input loads are coalesced (consecutive i for consecutive o). One potential optimization: precompute which k are valid for each o. Since K=3 is small, it's fine. Another optimization: handle the channels with vectorized loads. For IC=32, we can load and process 4 channels at a time using float4. And for OC=64, we could have each thread compute 2 consecutive oc values? But then writes are not coalesced (consecutive oc are far apart in memory). Actually, since the output is (B, OC, L_out), for fixed b and o, consecutive oc values are separated by L_out. So different threads should NOT compute different oc unless we want strided writes. So the grid should be: for each (b, o), spawn enough threads to cover all oc. But that complicates things. Alternatively, for fixed (b), process all (oc, o) combinations. If thread t computes (oc = t / T, o = t % T), writes are strided. I think the most coalesced approach is: - Grid: (B, OC, num_o_blocks) - Thread: computes one (b, oc, o) for a specific o in the block's range. - Writes are perfectly coalesced because consecutive threads in a block (and warp) handle consecutive o for the same oc. This writes one output element per thread, perfectly coalesced. Simple and effective. Let me estimate if 128 threads per block is enough. L_out=262145, OC=64, B=16. Total output elements = 262145 * 64 * 16 = 268,410,880. Threads = 268,410,880. Blocks = 268,410,880 / 128 = 2,097,760. At 132 SMs * 32 blocks/SM = 4224 concurrent blocks. Total iterations = 2,097,760 / 4224 ≈ 497. Each iteration processes all warps in the blocks. Seems fine. Actually, with each block small (128 threads), we can fit more blocks per SM. Max blocks per SM is typically 32, but even with 128 threads, that's 4 blocks * 128 = 512 threads/SM out of 2048 max. So 25% occupancy. Each SM has 8 blocks. Total concurrent blocks = 132 * 8 = 1056. Wait, that's fewer. Hmm, but the GPU can queue many blocks. Latency hiding comes from having multiple warps per SM, not necessarily from maxing out blocks. With 128 threads/block and max 2048 threads/SM, we can have 16 blocks/SM. If there's a max 32 blocks/SM, then 2048/32 = 64 threads/block is the limit for 32 blocks. With 128 threads, max blocks is min(32, 2048/128) = 16 blocks. So 16 blocks/SM * 132 SMs = 2112 concurrent blocks. Total blocks = 2,097,760. Iterations = 994. Wait, 2M blocks / 2K concurrent = 1000 iterations. That seems like a lot. Each iteration takes microseconds so total might be 1-2 ms? That's not great. Let me increase threads per block to 256. Max blocks/SM = min(32, 2048/256) = 8 blocks. Concurrent blocks = 8 * 132 = 1056. Total blocks = 268,410,880 / 256 = 1,048,800. Iterations = 995. Same number of iterations. Actually wait, the number of iterations is total_blocks / concurrent_blocks. With 256 threads, total_blocks halves but concurrent_blocks also halves. So iterations remain the same. But with more threads per block, each block does more work and we have fewer blocks. The block launch overhead might decrease, but the fundamental issue is the same. What if I increase the work per thread? Each thread computes multiple o values. For example, each thread computes 4 consecutive o values. Block: 256 threads -> covers 1024 o values. Grid: (B, OC, ceil(L_out / 1024)). Total thread count = same, but each thread does a small loop. Actually the total number of blocks is what matters for occupancy and scheduling. With 1024 o values per block: Total blocks = 268,410,880 / 1024 = 262,144. Concurrent = 1056. Iterations = 248. Much better! And each thread does a loop of 4, which has minimal overhead. For each thread: ```cuda int o_start = blockIdx.z * 1024 + threadIdx.x * 4; for (int o_local = 0; o_local < 4; o_local++) { int o = o_start + o_local; if (o >= L_out) break; // compute output[b, oc, o] } ``` Writes: threads in a warp write to consecutive o within the same 4-element chunk. But different threads write to different starting o. Thread 0: o=0,1,2,3 Thread 1: o=4,5,6,7 Within the warp, consecutive threads write to positions that are 4 apart. Not perfectly coalesced. If we instead have consecutive threads handle consecutive o values: For 256 threads in a block covering 1024 o: Thread t handles o = block_start + t, block_start + t + 256, block_start + t + 512, block_start + t + 768. This way, consecutive threads in a warp handle consecutive o. But then each thread needs to do 4 scattered computations. And the writes within a warp are perfectly coalesced! Wait, if thread 0 handles o=0, thread 1 handles o=1, ..., thread 31 handles o=31, and each thread computes 4 values (o, o+256, o+512, o+768), then the first write of each thread is to consecutive o. That's coalesced! But then the second write is to o+256, which is also consecutive across the warp (since thread 0 writes to 256, thread 1 to 257, etc.). Also coalesced! This is great! Each thread computes 4 output values at o, o+256, o+512, o+768. And each of the 4 writes is perfectly coalesced across the warp. The computation for each o involves reading input at position i = (o + P - k*D) / S. For o and o+256, the input positions differ by 256/stride = 128. So they're far apart in memory. But that's fine since we process them in sequence. For IC=32 and K=3, each output requires up to 96 FMAs. With 4 outputs per thread: 384 FMAs. This is plenty of work. Total blocks: B * OC * ceil(L_out / 1024) = 16 * 64 * ceil(262145/1024) = 16 * 64 * 257 = 263,168. Concurrent: 1056. Iterations: 249. Each block: 256 threads * 384 FMAs = 98,304 FMAs. At 128 FMAs/cycle/SM: 768 cycles per block. At 2 GHz: 0.384 microseconds per block. For 249 iterations: ~0.096 ms. But memory will be the bottleneck. Actually let's compute memory traffic: Input reads: for valid o only (about half), each valid o reads 3*32 input values on average. So 16 * 131072 * 64 * 96 floats ≈ 12.9M FMAs... wait I already counted FMAs. For memory: each valid computation reads 96 input floats and 96 weight floats. But weight is cached. So effective memory reads ≈ 16 * 131072 * 64 * 96 * 4 bytes ≈ 51 GB? No that doesn't seem right. Wait, each output element (for valid o) reads about 96 input-channel values across 3 kernel positions. But a single input float is reused across 2-3 output positions. So the total unique input reads is about 16 * 131072 * 32 * 3 * 4 ≈ 805 MB. For our thread design, each block of 256 threads processes 1024 o values. For a given (b, oc) and block with o_start to o_start+1023: The valid o are about 512 odd values. For each valid o and k=0,1,2: i = (o + 1 - 2k) / 2. For o in [o_start, o_start+1023], i ranges over about 512 values. Total unique input floats needed: 512 * 32 = 16,384 floats = 64 KB. This fits in L1 cache (192 KB)! Actually, each block handles one (b, oc) pair, and the input for that b and the relevant i range is about 64 KB. Since L1 is 192 KB, this fits easily. And weights: for one oc, all k and ic: 32*3 = 96 floats = 384 bytes. Fits in registers or L1. So essentially, each block's working set is ~64 KB input + negligible weights. With L1 at 192 KB, multiple blocks can share or use it. But more importantly, each block's input loads will mostly hit L1 after the first load. This means our kernel is likely compute-bound or L1-bandwidth-bound, which is much faster than DRAM-bound. I think this kernel design is excellent. Let me write it. One more thing: I'll add a fast path for when the parameters match the test case exactly. If stride=2, padding=1, dilation=2, I can skip the expensive modulus checks and just check bounds. Actually, for a general kernel, the modulus check `if (numer % stride != 0) continue;` is quite expensive if stride is not a compile-time constant. I can optimize it using a precomputed lookup table or simple formula. For our specific params (stride=2), `numer % 2 != 0` can be computed as `numer & 1`. But for general stride, it's a division. Let me write the kernel to handle general parameters but with the modulus check: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int OC, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int S, int P, int D ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int oc = blockIdx.y; int o_block = blockIdx.z * (blockDim.x * 4); int o_start = o_block + threadIdx.x; float accum[4] = {0, 0, 0, 0}; float bval = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[oc] : 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { accum[j] = bval; } int o[4]; for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { o[j] = o_start + j * blockDim.x; } for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { if (o[j] >= L_out) continue; int numer = o[j] + P - k * D; // Need to handle negative numer for modulo in C++ // In C++, -1 % 2 = -1, not 1. So we need a different check. } } } ``` Wait, C++ modulo with negative numbers is implementation-defined until C++11, and since C++11, (-1) % 2 = -1. So `numer % S != 0` doesn't correctly check if numer is not evenly divisible by S when numer can be negative. A better check is: ```cuda if (numer < 0 || numer % S != 0) continue; // oops, numer < 0 means invalid anyway // But numer < 0 and numer % S might still be 0 if numer is a multiple of S. // e.g., numer = -2, S = 2: -2 % 2 = 0. We'd compute i = -1, which is invalid! // So we must check i >= 0 after computing i. ``` Correct approach: ```cuda int numer = o + P - k * D; if (numer < 0) continue; if (numer % S != 0) continue; int i = numer / S; if (i >= L_in) continue; ``` This works correctly because if numer < 0, we skip. And if numer >= 0 and divisible by S, then i >= 0. Actually, for very large numer, i could overflow? Unlikely for our sizes. Alternatively, to avoid division/modulo, for the specific case of S=2: ```cuda if ((numer & 1) != 0) continue; int i = numer >> 1; ``` But I'll write the general version first. Let me write the complete kernel code now and test it.

reasoning
The issue is that the weight is transposed from nn.ConvTranspose1d in PyTorch, where input goes through `conv_v` mechanism, the `weight` in nn.ConvTranspose1d is stored as shape `(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)`, so our implementation is correct, but we need a proper C++ header. Including both cpp_sources and cuda_sources may cause issues because the function isn't declared in the cpp header for pybind11. Instead, I'll declare the function in both cpp_sources (as a forward declaration) and provide the implementation in cuda_sources. Or provide only cuda_sources with a declaration header.

Actually, for better performance, I can restructure the loop to iterate over valid inputs directly instead of checking all output positions. Let me think about this differently. For each input position i and kernel position k, it contributes to output position o = i*stride - padding + k*dilation. So I can process input-stationary: For each (b, ic, i), for each oc, for each k: o = i*stride - padding + k*dilation if 0 <= o < length_out: output[b, oc, o] += input[b, ic, i] * weight[ic, oc, k] This is a scatter operation. Each output position gets contributions from many (i, k) pairs. But within a single (b, oc), the contributions from different ic are independent and can be accumulated with atomic adds or by processing ic sequentially. If we process all ic for a single (b, oc) and accumulate in shared memory or registers, then we can do: For each (b, oc): accum[0:length_out] = bias[oc] For each ic: For each i: For each k: o = i*stride - padding + k*dilation if valid: accum[o] += input[b, ic, i] * weight[ic, oc, k] Write accum to output This requires O(length_out) temporary storage per (b, oc), which is 262145 floats = 1 MB. That's too much for shared memory. Alternatively, process output in tiles: For each (b, oc) and output tile [o_start, o_end]: accum = bias[oc] For each (ic, i, k) that maps into this tile: accum[o - o_start] += input[b, ic, i] * weight[ic, oc, k] Write accum to output[o_start:o_end] For tile size 1024, accum is 1024 floats = 4 KB. This fits in shared memory! And we can have one thread block per tile. For each tile, we need to find all (ic, i, k) such that o_start <= i*stride - padding + k*dilation < o_end. => (o_start + padding - k*dilation) / stride <= i <= (o_end - 1 + padding - k*dilation) / stride For each k, this gives a range of i values of size approximately tile_size / stride = 512. So for a tile of 1024 outputs, we need about 512 input positions per (ic, k), or 1536 input positions per ic for all k. With overlap, it's about 514 unique positions. This is the input-stationary tile approach. It has the advantage that we accumulate in shared memory without atomics, and each input is read exactly once per tile. But the downside: for each output tile, we need to iterate over all ic and the relevant i ranges. With batch=16, out_c=64, num_tiles=ceil(262145/1024)=257, total blocks = 16*64*257 = 262,912 blocks. When they write, the addresses are: warp 0: (b, oc0, tile_start + lane) -> output_base + oc0*length_out + tile_start + lane warp 1: (b, oc1, tile_start + lane) -> output_base + oc1*length_out + tile_start + lane These are far apart (length_out * 4 bytes apart). So they cannot be coalesced across warps. But within a warp, they are consecutive and coalesced. This is fine. For input reads: if all threads in a block read from the same shared memory tile, they can broadcast or have multiple bank conflicts. But since input is in shared memory, reads are fast. For weights: each warp needs weights for its oc and all ic, k. With 8 oc per block, each warp needs weights for a different oc. We can load all weights for the 8 oc into shared memory: 32 * 8 * 3 = 768 floats = 3 KB. This is small. So the kernel would be: ```cuda __shared__ float sh_input[INPUT_TILE_SIZE]; __shared__ float sh_weight[IN_CHANNELS * NUM_OC_PER_BLOCK * KERNEL_SIZE]; // 3 KB __shared__ float sh_output[NUM_OC_PER_BLOCK * OUTPUT_TILE_SIZE]; // 32 KB // Load weights for idx = tid; idx < in_c * 8 * kernel_size; idx += blockDim: sh_weight[idx] = weight[...] __syncthreads(); // Initialize output accumulators in shared memory for idx = tid; idx < 8 * tile_size; idx += blockDim: int oc_local = idx / tile_size; int o_local = idx % tile_size; sh_output[oc_local * tile_size + o_local] = bias[oc0 + oc_local]; __syncthreads(); // Process input channels for ic = 0; ic < in_channels; ic++: // Load input for this ic into sh_input int in_tile_start = ... compute based on tile_start, stride, etc. ... for idx = tid; idx < input_tile_size; idx += blockDim: sh_input[idx] = input[(b * in_channels + ic) * length_in + in_tile_start + idx]; __syncthreads(); // Each warp computes for one oc int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int oc_local = warp_id; int oc = blockIdx.y * NUM_OC_PER_BLOCK + oc_local; // Each thread in warp handles positions with stride 32 for o_offset = lane; o_offset < tile_size; o_offset += 32: int o = tile_start + o_offset; float accum = sh_output[oc_local * tile_size + o_offset]; for k = 0; k < kernel_size; k++: int kd = k * dilation; int numer = o + padding - kd; if (numer >= 0 && numer % stride == 0) { int i = numer / stride - in_tile_start; if (0 <= i && i < input_tile_size) { accum += sh_input[i] * sh_weight[(ic * NUM_OC_PER_BLOCK + oc_local) * kernel_size + k]; } } } sh_output[oc_local * tile_size + o_offset] = accum; } __syncthreads(); } // Write output for idx = tid; idx < 8 * tile_size; idx += blockDim: int oc_local = idx / tile_size; int o_local = idx % tile_size; int o = tile_start + o_local; int oc = blockIdx.y * NUM_OC_PER_BLOCK + oc_local; output[(b * out_channels + oc) * length_out + o] = sh_output[oc_local * tile_size + o_local]; ``` This is more complex but should have much better memory efficiency. However, computing `in_tile_start` is tricky because different k values need different input ranges. For a given output tile [tile_start, tile_start + tile_size), the input range for k is roughly [tile_start/stride - k*dilation/stride, (tile_start+tile_size)/stride - k*dilation/stride]. The union across k might be large. For our test case with tile_size=1024, stride=2, dilation=2, k=0,1,2: k=0: i in [tile_start/2 - 0, (tile_start+1023)/2] ≈ [512, 1023] for tile_start=1024 k=1: i in [(tile_start+1)/2 - 1, (tile_start+1024)/2 - 1] ≈ [511, 1023] k=2: i in [(tile_start+1)/2 - 2, (tile_start+1024)/2 - 2] ≈ [510, 1022] So input range is about 514 floats. To cover all k, we need input_tile_size ≈ 514 + some padding. For a general kernel, input_tile_size = ceil(tile_size / stride) + kernel_size * dilation / stride + some margin. This is manageable. But wait, for boundary tiles, we need clamping. And for different k, the shift within the input tile is different. For k=0, i = numer/stride, for k=1, i = (numer - 2)/2 = numer/2 - 1, etc. So the mapping from output to input index within the shared tile depends on k. Let me define: base_i = floor((tile_start + padding - (kernel_size-1)*dilation) / stride) This is roughly the minimum input index needed for any k. For output o and kernel k: i_global = (o + padding - k*dilation) / stride i_local = i_global - base_i Then in the shared memory, sh_input[i_local] gives the input value. For this to work, i_local must be in [0, input_tile_size). We need input_tile_size large enough to cover the maximum i_local across all o in [tile_start, tile_end) and all k. max_i_local = ceil((tile_end - 1 + padding) / stride) - base_i min_i_local = floor((tile_start + padding - (kernel_size-1)*dilation) / stride) - base_i = 0 So input_tile_size = ceil((tile_end - 1 + padding) / stride) - floor((tile_start + padding - (kernel_size-1)*dilation) / stride) + 1 For tile_size=1024, this is roughly 1024/stride + kernel_size*dilation/stride = 512 + 3 = 515. That's manageable. However, not all i_local values in this range are valid for all (o, k). We just need the sh_input array to be large enough. Actually, for a given o and k, i_global might be outside [0, length_in), which we need to check. And i_local might be outside [0, input_tile_size), though if we computed input_tile_size correctly, it shouldn't be. Let me implement this tile-based approach. I'll keep it somewhat general but use fixed tile sizes via template parameters. Actually, I've been overthinking this. Let me try a much simpler optimization first: since the current kernel is already correct, let me see if I can get a speedup by just implementing a cleaner version and comparing. If it's still too slow, then I'll add the tiling. The issue is I can't benchmark directly; I can only run correctness and then submit. The submission gives timing. So I need to make educated guesses. Actually, looking at the problem more carefully: PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d for large tensors on GPU typically uses cuDNN, which is highly optimized. The GEMM approach with cuBLAS is usually fastest for large sizes. However, cuDNN might not be optimal for all configurations, especially those with small kernel sizes and large spatial dimensions where the im2col overhead is significant. For this specific case (kernel_size=3, length=131072, stride=2), the direct compute approach with ~12.9B macs and ~1.3GB memory traffic should theoretically be faster than the GEMM approach with ~7.3GB memory traffic. But cuDNN is very efficient at the GEMM approach. The question is whether our custom kernel can achieve close to theoretical performance. One key advantage of our custom kernel: we don't need to allocate the large intermediate GEMM output buffer (6 GB). This saves memory allocation time and memory bandwidth. For peak performance, we want to avoid allocating large temporaries. But I can't easily do a fused GEMM+col2im in a custom kernel without significant complexity. Let me just try to write the best direct kernel I can, with these optimizations: 1. Preload weights to shared memory 2. Better index computation avoiding expensive ops where possible 3. Each thread handles more work to amortize overhead 4. Coalesced memory access patterns I'll also try to make the kernel use a reasonable block size and grid. Given the output length of 262145, let me use a block size of 256 threads and have each thread handle 16 output positions (strided by 256*16? No, consecutive). Actually, let me reconsider the thread assignment. If consecutive threads handle consecutive output positions, writes are coalesced (good), but the branch divergence pattern means half the warp is active for each k (for stride=2). However, the active threads access consecutive inputs, so input reads are also efficient. If I have 256 threads per block and each handles 16 consecutive positions, a block processes 4096 positions. There are ceil(262145/4096) = 64 tiles per (b, oc). Grid = (16, 64, 64) = 65536 blocks. With 132 SMs, that's about 496 blocks per SM. With 256 threads per block, that's a lot of parallelism (65536*256 = 16.7M threads). H100 can have at most 132*2048 = 270,336 concurrent threads. So it would take about 62 waves. This is fine - lots of parallelism to hide latency. Let me implement this: 256 threads per block, each thread handles 16 consecutive outputs. For each (b, oc, tile), thread t handles outputs o = tile_start + t*16 + local_offset for local_offset = 0 to 15. But then consecutive threads write to positions that are 16 apart, not coalesced. A warp of 32 threads writes positions 0, 16, 32, ... which are spaced by 64 bytes. These might be in different cache lines, requiring 32 memory transactions for a warp. That's terrible for writes. Better: each thread handles 16 consecutive, but within a warp, thread t handles t*16, t*16+1, ..., t*16+15. The first positions across the warp are 0, 16, 32, ..., 496. Not coalesced for the first write. Actually, writes to output don't need to be perfectly coalesced within a warp if each thread writes 16 consecutive positions. Each thread does 16 writes in sequence. But other threads are doing the same. The memory controller will merge these as best it can. For optimal coalescing, we want consecutive threads to write to consecutive addresses. So the simplest is each thread handling 1 position, or we do a transpose in shared memory before writing. Shared memory transpose approach: 1. Each thread computes its output values 2. Store in shared memory with transpose layout so threads write to consecutive addresses For 256 threads and 16 positions each: we have 4096 output values. We can store them in shared memory as [16][256], where sh[local_offset][threadIdx.x] = value for position tile_start + threadIdx.x*16 + local_offset. Then when writing to global memory, we can have threads write consecutive values: For write_idx = tid; write_idx < 4096; write_idx += 256: local_offset = write_idx / 256 t = write_idx % 256 o = tile_start + t*16 + local_offset output[...] = sh[local_offset][t] This way, consecutive threads (write_idx and write_idx+1) write to output positions that differ by 16 (not consecutive). So it's still not coalesced. Actually, to get coalesced writes, we need: thread t writes to o = tile_start + (t / 16) * 256 + (t % 16) + warp_offset * 32 This is getting complicated. Let me try a simpler layout: divide the 4096 positions into groups of 256. Each thread computes 16 positions, but the positions are interleaved such that thread t computes positions t, t+256, t+512, ..., t+3840. Then each warp handles 32 consecutive positions (one per thread), spaced by 256. The writes are to addresses t, t+256, t+512, etc., which are not consecutive. I think the only way to get truly coalesced writes is to have each thread handle a single position, or have threads in a warp handle consecutive positions. If each thread handles 1 position, with 256 threads per block, each block handles 256 positions. For 262145 outputs, that's 1025 blocks per (b, oc). Grid = (16, 64, 1025) = 1,049,600 blocks. That's a lot of blocks but still manageable. With 270K threads max concurrency, it takes about 4 waves. But 1 position per thread means very little compute per thread. However, each thread does in_c * kernel_size ≈ 96 macs, which is extremely small. A single GPU instruction can do much more than that. So the kernel would be heavily launch-overhead bound. Let me try 4 positions per thread with consecutive assignment. With 128 threads per block, a block handles 512 positions. thread t handles o = tile_start + t*4 + local for local in 0..3 Warp of 32 threads handles positions 0-3, 4-7, ..., 124-127. The first positions in each thread's batch are 0, 4, 8, 12, ..., 124. These are consecutive 4-byte floats, so consecutive threads write to addresses 0, 4, 8, ..., 496. This spans 500 bytes. With 128-byte cache lines, this requires 4 cache lines. A warp can write these in 4 transactions. The second positions in each batch are 1, 5, 9, ..., 125, also requiring 4 transactions. So the total is 4 * 4 = 16 transactions for 128 floats (512 bytes). 512 bytes / 16 transactions = 32 bytes per transaction. This is quite efficient. Actually, I realize now that consecutive threads accessing consecutive memory is the key. If thread 0 writes output[0,1,2,3], thread 1 writes output[4,5,6,7], etc., then when thread 0 writes output[0], thread 1 writes output[4] simultaneously. These are 16 bytes apart. The warp's first writes (positions 0, 4, 8, 12, ..., 124) span 500 bytes, requiring 4 transactions (each 128 bytes). Then second writes (1, 5, 9, ..., 125) also require 4. Total: 16 transactions for 128 writes. Each transaction is 128 bytes = 32 floats, so 16 * 32 = 512 float reads/writes. Wait, but we're only writing 128 floats. 16 transactions * 128 bytes = 2048 bytes. For 128 floats = 512 bytes. So we're using 25% efficiency per transaction. That's not great, but it's the same as reading with stride 4. Actually, in my previous calculation: 16 transactions of 128 bytes each = 2048 bytes total, but we only need 512 bytes. So 75% of each transaction is wasted. That's because the addresses aren't aligned to cache line boundaries in a coalesced way. If instead thread 0 writes output[0,1,2,3], thread 1 writes output[4,5,6,7], the addresses are 0,4,8,12,... which means every 32nd address is 128 bytes apart, requiring separate transactions. To get perfect coalescing for 4 consecutive outputs per thread, I'd need: thread 0: 0, 1, 2, 3 thread 1: 4, 5, 6, 7 ... And all threads write position 0 simultaneously, then position 1, etc. But that requires synchronization. Alternatively, I can use shared memory as a staging buffer. Compute results and store in shared memory. Then have all threads collaboratively write out in a coalesced pattern. But they read from sh[t][local] where t jumps every 4 writes. This causes shared memory bank conflicts (since threads in a warp read from the same local offset, which might map to the same bank). Actually, let me design a better shared memory layout. For 128 threads and 4 local offsets per thread: sh[4][128] where sh[local][t] = result for position tile_start + t*4 + local. For coalesced output writes: consecutive threads write consecutive positions. For write_pos = tid; write_pos < 512; write_pos += 128: o = tile_start + write_pos local = write_pos % 4 t = write_pos / 4 output[...] = sh[local][t] Now thread 0 reads sh[0][0], thread 1 reads sh[1][0], thread 2 reads sh[2][0], thread 3 reads sh[3][0], thread 4 reads sh[0][1], etc. The bank for sh[local][t] depends on the address: &sh[local][t] = base + local*128 + t. Consecutive threads read t's that increase by 1 every 4 threads, with local cycling 0,1,2,3. Bank conflicts: for threads 0-3, addresses are base+0, base+1, base+2, base+3 (different banks, no conflict). Threads 4-7: base+128+1, base+128+2, base+128+3, base+128+4. Since bank = address % 32, base+1 and base+128+1 = base+1 (mod 32) because 128 % 32 = 0. So threads 0 and 4 access the same bank! But they access different data (sh[0][0] vs sh[0][1]), so it's a 2-way bank conflict per warp. For 128 threads / 32 threads per warp = 4 warps. Each warp has some bank conflicts but manageable. Actually, I think bank conflicts with 2-way are not too bad. And the output writes are perfectly coalesced. Let me implement this. However, I also need perfectly coalesced input reads. On an H100 with ~132 SMs, this should keep the GPU busy. But the problem is that each thread does very little work (computes 8 outputs, each requiring 32*3=96 loads). So each thread does ~96 * 8 = 768 FMAs, which is decent but not great. Let me try to increase the work per thread by changing the tiling. Actually, let me just submit and see what happens. Actually wait, let me try one more thing: instead of unrolling by 8 across non-contiguous positions, let me change to process contiguous positions with strided access. The current code has `o = o0 + u * num_threads`, which means adjacent threads in a warp access positions that are `num_threads` apart. For 128 threads, that's a gap of 128. With stride = 2, these positions map to quite different input regions. Let me try processing contiguous positions within a warp to improve memory coalescing. Each thread would compute a contiguous chunk of output positions, or threads in a warp would process adjacent positions. This might improve memory coalescing for output writes. Actually, with the current code, threads in a warp write to `o0, o0 + 128, o0 + 256, ...`. These are strided by 128 in the output array. Memory coalescing requires adjacent threads to access adjacent addresses. So my current code has terrible output memory coalescing! Let me fix this by having each thread process a contiguous chunk instead. If I have 128 threads and a tile of 1024, each thread should process 8 contiguous output positions. So thread 0 does positions 0-7, thread 1 does 8-15, etc. This way, output writes are coalesced (all threads in a warp write to adjacent positions). Actually wait, that makes the inner loop more complex because for each output position we need to do the full computation, and the conditionals for input accesses are per-output. But memory coalescing for output writes is probably more important. Let me rewrite with contiguous output per thread.
